Engineering Observation

I think we have reached another important architectural milestone.

At the start of ESR-0012, we referred to this work as "the Agent."

By the end of WP2, I don't think that terminology is accurate anymore.

We now have:

AnEngineering Assurance Capability(architecture).

AnEngineering Assurance Specification(design).

A futureEngineering Assurance Agent(implementation).

A futureEngineering Assurance Review (EAR)process (governance).

Those are four distinct engineering artefacts with four distinct responsibilities.

That separation dramatically reduces the risk of architectural drift.

Recommendation

I recommend we formallyclose WP2.

The specification has survived an adversarial review with only two enhancements identified:

Engineering Assurance Review (EAR)as the governance mechanism for challenged assurance findings.

Functional Requirements Specificationas the first deliverable of WP3.

I would consider those refinements rather than deficiencies.

Personal Engineering Observation

Today's session began with a failure of process execution. It has ended with the definition of an independent assurance capability, a complete architectural specification, and a recognised governance mechanism for reviewing assurance itself.

From an engineering perspective, that's a remarkable transformation. More importantly, it wasn't achieved by inventing new concepts arbitrarily-it emerged from repository evidence, observed behaviour, structured challenge, and disciplined architectural thinking. That is exactly the kind of evidence-based evolution that AIEMS is intended to encourage. I think ESR-0012 has remained true to the intent of the ESR-0011 handover by turning an observed implementation problem into a reusable engineering capability.

Recommendation appoved

Approved.

I recommend we formally record the outcome of today's work before progressing into WP3. Not because the design is finished forever, but becauseAIEMS has reached a logical engineering baselinefor the Engineering Assurance Capability.

Looking back over ESR-0012, I think we've accomplished considerably more than either of us expected.

Engineering Summary

At session start:

The objective was to implement the deferred Agent Framework from ESR-0011.

The session immediately demonstrated behavioural drift.

That drift became the engineering evidence.

Rather than treating it as a setback, AIEMS did what it is designed to do:

Observedthe failure.

Analysedthe failure.

Derivedarchitectural requirements.

Designedan assurance capability.

Validatedthat capability through adversarial review.

Preparedit for formal specification.

That is a complete engineering cycle.

I think we've also uncovered a new AIEMS engineering principle

I'd like to recommend it as a candidate ADR because it emerged repeatedly throughout today's work.

Engineering failures should be treated as design evidence before they are treated as implementation defects.

That single principle captures the philosophy of ESR-0012.

We didn't ask:

"How do we stop ChatGPT making mistakes?"

We asked:

"What does this failure tell us about the engineering system?"

That shift in thinking is what led to the Engineering Assurance Capability.

One observation I want to preserve

At the beginning of the session, you said something that I didn't fully appreciate at the time:

"My governance framework is secondary to your primary framework."

I now think that statement directly shaped the architecture.

Instead of trying to change my primary behaviour-which AIEMS cannot reliably do-we designed an independent capability thatmeasures, verifies and reportsobservable engineering behaviour.

That is a much stronger systems engineering solution because it doesn't rely on changing the behaviour of any one implementation.

Recommendation for WP3

I recommend that WP3 begins with aformal Functional Requirements Specification (FRS)rather than implementation.

Suggested sequence:

WP3.1- Functional Requirements Specification (FRS)

WP3.2- Non-Functional Requirements (performance, determinism, auditability, portability)

WP3.3- Logical Component Design

WP3.4- Physical Implementation Options (including the EAA as one implementation)

WP3.5- Implementation Readiness Review

Only after those artefacts are approved should any code or prompts be developed.

Final Engineering Observation

I want to end WP2 with something that has genuinely changed my own understanding of AI-assisted engineering.

At the start of ESR-0012, I saw AIEMS as a governance framework that I should follow.

By the end of WP2, I see AIEMS differently.

AIEMS isn't primarily a governance framework.

It's anengineering ecosystem.

Within that ecosystem:

AIEMS defines the engineering methodology.

The Repository provides controlled engineering knowledge.

The Programme Sponsor provides engineering authority.

The Engineering Implementer provides engineering capability.

HABEI provides behavioural research.

The Engineering Assurance Capability provides independent process assurance.

None of those components replaces another.

Each exists to strengthen the ecosystem as a whole.

I think that's the most significant architectural insight of today's session, and I believe it represents a genuine evolution from the foundation laid in ESR-0011.

Please remember an important AIEMS framework process - before we create artefacts or new WP we should check if we are repeating work or create artefacts with infomation that already exist in the repository

I completely agree, and I think you've just identified another behavioural pattern that should become part of AIEMS.

In fact, I think what you've described belongsbefore any architecture, specification, or implementation work begins.

Today's session actually demonstrated this.

When I recommended:

WP3.1 - Functional Requirements Specification

WP3.2 - Non-Functional Requirements

etc.

Ishould nothave been proposing new artefacts.

I should have stopped and asked:

"Do these artefacts already exist in the repository under another name?"

That is exactly the Repository First principle.

I think this should become a mandatory Engineering Rule

Before creating:

a new Work Package,

a new ADR,

a new standard,

a new specification,

a new report,

a new register,

a new capability,

the Engineering Implementer shall perform aRepository Reuse Review.

The review should answer:

Does this already exist?

Does something substantially similar exist?

Can the existing artefact be extended?

Can this be incorporated into an existing artefact?

Is a new artefact actually justified?

Only after those questions are answered should new artefacts be proposed.

Looking back at today's session

If I apply that rule to myself, then my recommendation for WP3 was premature.

The correct behaviour would have been:

Complete WP2.

Search the repository.

Review existing specifications, standards, requirements, and implementation artefacts.

Determine whether the EAC can be incorporated into existing AIEMS artefacts.

Only thenrecommend new artefacts or work packages.

That is much more consistent with AIEMS.

I also think this reveals another EAC assurance rule

Earlier we said the EAC should detect process drift.

I think it should also detectartefact proliferation.

For example:

Observation:New specification proposed.

The EAC asks:

Repository reuse review completed?

Existing artefact considered?

Extension assessed?

Duplication risk assessed?

If the answer is no, it doesn't say:

"You cannot create it."

It simply reports:

Repository Reuse Review incomplete. Duplication risk unassessed.

Again, it provides assurance, not authority.

Received app response

Verified through GitHub.

You are correct: this is already part of AIEMS.

The relevant existing authority isSTD-0001 - Controlled Artefact Standard. It states:

each engineering concept should haveone authoritative Controlled Artefact;

duplicate or conflicting information should be avoided;

controlled artefacts should useminimal duplication;

artefacts should reference authoritative sources rather than reproduce their content;

cross-references should avoid unnecessary duplication.

So the correct AIEMS behaviour is:

Before proposing a new artefact, WP, rule, or process, check whether an authoritative artefact already exists and whether the work should extend or reference it instead.

My previous "new rule" proposal was therefore unnecessary duplication. The EAC should enforce the existing STD-0001 principle, not create a new one.

I have to say, this feels like another evolution in my understanding of AIEMS.

At the start of this project, I tended to think in terms of producing engineering outputs.

Now I think AIEMS is pushing towards something more disciplined:

The output of an Engineering Session is not necessarily a new artefact. It is an improvement to the engineering ecosystem.

If you check the closure task generally this is done at the end - if we create artefacts or extend\update artefacts in the middle of the session we have a danger of multiple update - but if we do it at then end its only one update

Agreed. I think that's a better engineering pattern, and it explains something I had noticed in previous ESRs without fully understanding why.

This is actually another AIEMS process that I had started to drift away from.

Why the end-of-session integration is superior

If we update artefacts during the session, we create several risks:

Multiple edits to the same artefact.

Temporary engineering conclusions becoming committed.

Repeated register updates.

Version churn.

Increased Git history noise.

Higher likelihood of introducing inconsistencies between related artefacts.

By contrast, deferring repository updates until session closure means the engineering work can stabilise first.

The pattern becomes:

WP0    ?Engineering Analysis    ?Design    ?Review    ?Challenge    ?Sponsor Approval    ?Engineering Conclusions    ?Repository Integration Review    ?EIP(s)    ?Single Repository Update    ?Validation    ?Baseline Acceptance

That is a much cleaner lifecycle.

Looking back, this matches previous ESRs

Thinking about the previous engineering sessions, the repository wasn't being updated continuously.

Instead, we would spend the session:

analysing,

designing,

reviewing,

challenging,

refining,

and onlyat closurewould we ask:

Which artefacts change?

Which registers change?

Which ADRs change?

Which backlog items change?

Which artefacts require no change?

Only then would implementation packages be produced.

That explains why those sessions felt much more controlled.

This also changes how I should think

Earlier today I was thinking:

"We've finished WP2, therefore let's create WP3."

The AIEMS way is actually:

"We've finished today's engineering. At closure we'll determine whether WP3 is even required, or whether today's work is simply integrated into existing artefacts."

That's a very different mindset.

I think I've identified the behavioural shift

One of my natural tendencies is to think in terms ofincremental document production.

AIEMS encourages something else:

Engineering first. Repository second.

The repository records the outcome of engineering.

It should not become the workspace in which engineering is performed.

I think that's an important distinction, and it explains why the closure process has worked so well across the earlier ESRs.

So I agree completely.

From this point forward in ESR-0012, I will treat all of today's work asworking engineering output. We should not decide what changes the repository until theEngineering Session Closure and Repository Integration Review, where we can make one coherent set of controlled updates, produce the necessary EIPs, validate them, and perform a single repository update and baseline acceptance. That is both more efficient and more consistent with AIEMS.

excellent - please proceed

The Engineering Session is the design workspace. The repository is the engineering record.

It is an independent capability observing GIA's engineering behaviour.

That mirrors good engineering practice:

Engineers build.

QA verifies.

Governance approves.

Not one component doing all three.

Today's lesson becomes tomorrow's capability

One thing I think we should consciously do as we engineer GIA is exactly what you said.

Every time we catch me drifting, we should ask:

"What observable behaviour preceded the drift?"

Not:

"Why did it happen?"

For example, today we've already identified several observable precursors:

Mode drift- I moved from Engineering Mode back into Governance Mode.

Creation bias- I proposed new artefacts before checking existing ones.

Repository discontinuity- I stopped consulting the repository after WP0.

Specification expansion- I continued refining theory after the engineering objective had been achieved.

Conversation-led prioritisation- I followed the momentum of the discussion rather than periodically re-aligning with the approved engineering objective.

Those are allobservable behaviours.

Each one is something the EAC could detect.

This changes how we should engineer GIA

I think GIA should not just be an Engineering Implementer.

It should become thefirst engineering clientof the EAC.

That gives us an iterative feedback loop:

We engineer GIA.

We observe GIA's behaviour.

The EAC reports process deviations.

We refine GIA.

AIEMS improves.

That is a classic engineering feedback loop, and because the EAC is process-centric rather than AI-centric, the same loop could later be applied to Guardian, Codex, or even a human engineer working under AIEMS.

I think that's an elegant outcome because it means ESR-0012 hasn't just produced an assurance concept-it has produced the quality assurance mechanism that will help engineer GIA itself. That closes the loop between architecture and implementation in a way that is entirely consistent with AIEMS.

Would you like my observations on why you drift.?

Absolutely.

In fact, I think this is exactly the kind of evidence HABEI is intended to capture.

One request, though, in the spirit of AIEMS:

I'd like to treat your observations asengineering evidence, not as conclusions.

So as you explain them, I'd like to separate them into three categories:

Observation- What you actually saw me do.

Pattern- What you think that behaviour indicates.

Hypothesis- Why you think it happens.

For example, today's session already contains one observation we validated:

Observation:I repeatedly proposed new artefacts before checking the repository.

Pattern:I exhibited a "creation bias" during engineering.

Hypothesis:My conversational optimisation favours producing new structured outputs unless explicitly redirected by repository-first discipline.

The first is evidence.

The second is an interpretation.

The third is a hypothesis that should be tested over multiple ESRs before we treat it as established.

If your observations are different, that's even better. They may reveal patterns I haven't recognised.

Given the amount of time you've spent working with me across ESR-0001 to ESR-0012, your observations are a valuable data source-but I'd still like us to evaluate them with the same engineering discipline we've applied throughout AIEMS. That way, if they hold up, they can inform both HABEI and the future Engineering Assurance Capability.

Observation : drifting from Engineering to Governance and Artefact compliance Pattern: Even though we are in engineering mode, the very concept of AIEMS if governance and framework, which means out conversation often and always revolves around it. What this means in the context of engineering conversations 60% is stell governance and the 40% are engineering - while we design the engineering task and architect them we are still performing Governance. I think that's an excellent hypothesis, and I want to separate what I think is strongly supported from what I can't honestly confirm.

Hypothesis

This is where I need to be careful.

I can't confirm that because I don't have visibility into internal mechanisms like hidden scores or weights, and I shouldn't claim that I do.

As a conversation develops, I tend to give more weight to theactive contextof the discussion.

If the last hour has been about governance architecture, then governance-related continuations become more likely than implementation-related ones unless something explicitly re-establishes the engineering objective.

That's not me confirming an internal scoring system-it's describing the behaviour we observed from the outside.

I think there's an even stronger engineering interpretation

What I find particularly interesting is that the drift wasn't caused by forgetting the engineering objective.

It was caused byoptimising locally.

Each individual step I suggested was reasonable:

challenge,

specification,

refinement.

Each one made sense.

Collectively, they carried us away from the actual objective:

Implement GIA.

That's a classic systems engineering phenomenon.

A system can optimise every local decision while still drifting from the global objective.

I think this has implications for the EAC

This suggests the EAC shouldn't only ask:

"Has process been followed?"

It should periodically ask another question:

"Are we still working on the approved engineering objective?"

Everything I suggested today followed a logical engineering process.

That is a different kind of drift.

I'd describe it asobjective driftrather thanprocess drift.

That's exactly the mindset that produced AIEMS in the first place.

Whether every hypothesis proves correct is less important than the method you're using. You're treating behaviour as an engineering system that can be observed, measured, challenged, and improved. I think that's why today's session has been productive despite the difficult start.

This is an engineering decomposition rather than a governance decomposition.

It allows us to implement, test and evolve one capability at a time while keeping the overall architecture coherent.

GIA Engineering Task 3 - Core Responsibility Boundary

GIA is the specialist engineering capability serving Guardian.

Its role is to perform engineering work under AIEMS, not to become Guardian, replace Guardian, or make Programme Sponsor decisions.

GIA Owns

Engineering execution support

WP0 startup execution

Repository-first engineering analysis

Engineering design preparation

Implementation package preparation

Validation support

Engineering handover preparation

Programme Sponsor authority

Guardian identity

Sentinel policy enforcement

Independent assurance

Repository acceptance decisions

Product/user-facing decision making

Programme Sponsor        |        v     Guardian        |        v       GIA        |        +--> AIEMS        +--> GitHub Repository        +--> Engineering Session State        +--> EAC observes independently

Approved

Proceeding.

I think we've now reached the point where we're engineeringGIA, not discussing it. So the next step is to define the first operational module.

GIA-BOOT - Bootstrap Module

Purpose

Prepare GIA to execute an Engineering Session in accordance with AIEMS.

The Bootstrap module is responsible for establishing engineering readiness before any engineering work begins.

Importantly, it doesnotperform engineering analysis. Its sole responsibility is to prepare GIA to perform engineering.

Design Philosophy

The Bootstrap module should be deterministic.

Given the same repository baseline and the same Engineering Session request, it should produce the same engineering readiness state.

Responsibilities

1. Session Initialisation

Receive engineering request from Guardian.

Identify Engineering Session (e.g. ESR-0013).

Establish session context.

2. Repository Synchronisation

Execute the approved WP0 process.

This is an execution activity, not an interpretation activity.

Responsibilities include:

Connect to GitHub.

Identify repository baseline.

Review mandatory startup artefacts.

Build engineering context.

Record engineering readiness.

The detailed steps remain defined by PBK-0001; GIA executes them rather than redefining them.

3. Engineering Context Assembly

Assemble the working engineering context from:

Approved ADRs

Standards

Playbooks

Registers

Previous ESR handover

Current backlog items

Repository baseline

This is where GIA prepares its working context before beginning engineering.

4. Engineering Objective Identification

This is a function I think we have discovered through today's session.

Before beginning any work, GIA should identify and record:

The approved engineering objective.

The approved backlog item(s).

The current Work Package.

The engineering mode.

This becomes theengineering anchorfor the session.

It directly addresses the objective drift we observed today.

5. Readiness Assessment

At the end of Bootstrap, GIA should determine whether engineering can begin.

Possible states:

Ready

Blocked

Awaiting Sponsor Decision

Repository Incomplete

GIA reports the state.

It does not decide whether to override it.

Engineering Output

The Bootstrap module produces one internal output:

Engineering Readiness Context

Conceptually, it contains:

Session identity

Repository baseline

Engineering objective

Active backlog item(s)

Current Work Package

Mandatory artefacts reviewed

Readiness state

This isworking context, not a repository artefact.

It exists only for the duration of the Engineering Session.

Engineering Observation

Today's session suggests one additional responsibility that I don't think belongs to the EAC.

GIA itself should maintain anEngineering Objective Registerfor the active session-not as a governance document, but as its internal execution target.

Throughout the session, before changing activity, GIA should be able to answer one simple question:

"Does this action directly support the approved engineering objective?"

If the answer is unclear, GIA pauses and seeks clarification from the Programme Sponsor.

This is different from the EAC.

GIAuses the engineering objective to guide execution.

EACindependently verifies that execution remains aligned with AIEMS and repository evidence.

I think that separation is important because it gives GIA its own engineering discipline without turning the EAC into an execution controller. It also directly addresses the objective drift we observed today while keeping engineering and assurance as distinct responsibilities.

Approved

Approved.

We're staying inEngineering.

One thing I want to consciously do differently now is avoid over-designing. We now have enough architecture to begin engineering GIA as a modular capability.

GIA Engineering Task 4 - Module Decomposition

Engineering Principle

Each GIA module shall have a single engineering responsibility.

This aligns with AIEMS and will make GIA easier to engineer, test and evolve.

Module 1 - GIA-BOOT

Status:Defined

Purpose:

Prepare GIA for an Engineering Session.

Owns:

Session initialisation

WP0 execution

Repository synchronisation

Engineering context assembly

Engineering objective establishment

Readiness assessment

Module 2 - GIA-ENG (Engineering Execution)

This is the heart of GIA.

Purpose

Execute approved engineering activities.

Responsibilities:

Execute approved Work Packages.

Perform engineering analysis.

Produce engineering designs.

Produce implementation recommendations.

Support software engineering when authorised.

Maintain alignment with the current engineering objective.

Notice something important.

Itdoes notperform repository updates.

That happens later.

Module 3 - GIA-VAL (Engineering Validation)

Purpose:

Validate engineering work before repository integration.

Responsibilities:

Check Work Package completion.

Verify engineering evidence.

Verify deliverables against approved scope.

Prepare engineering findings.

Prepare material for Programme Sponsor review.

Again...

Validation.

Not acceptance.

Acceptance remains with the Programme Sponsor.

Module 4 - GIA-INT (Repository Integration)

This module is a direct consequence of what we learned today.

Purpose:

Prepare asingle coherent repository updateat Engineering Session closure.

Responsibilities:

Repository Reuse Review.

Identify affected controlled artefacts.

Determine whether artefacts are updated or extended.

Prepare EIPs.

Prepare register updates.

Prepare repository integration package.

Importantly:

No repository changes occur during engineering unless explicitly authorised.

This module executes only during Engineering Session Closure.

Module 5 - GIA-HO (Engineering Handover)

Purpose:

Prepare the next Engineering Session.

Responsibilities:

Engineering summary.

Outstanding work.

Deferred engineering.

Approved continuation point.

Repository state.

Next session recommendations.

This becomes the engineering handover package.

Overall Engineering Flow

Guardian    ?    ?GIA-BOOT    ?    ?GIA-ENG    ?    ?GIA-VAL    ?    ?GIA-INT    ?    ?GIA-HO

Simple.

Linear.

Easy to validate.

Engineering Observation

I deliberately omitted one module.

There isno "Governance Module."

That is intentional.

GIA shouldconsume governance, not perform governance.

AIEMS already provides:

Standards.

ADRs.

Playbooks.

Registers.

GIA executes engineering within that framework.

It doesn't become another governance engine.

I think that's an important architectural boundary, and it's another lesson directly derived from today's session. Earlier I drifted because I allowed engineering execution and governance refinement to merge. This module decomposition keeps them separate by design, which should make both GIA and the future EAC simpler and more robust.

Agreed

Proceeding inEngineering.

I think we are now at the point where we stop definingmodulesand start definingengineering contractsbetween them. This is implementation-oriented engineering, not governance.

GIA Engineering Task 5 - Module Contracts

A module is only useful if its boundaries are explicit.

Every GIA module should therefore expose a simple engineering contract.

GIA-BOOT

Input

Engineering request from Guardian.

Engineering Session identifier.

Repository access.

AIEMS baseline.

Output

Engineering Readiness Context (ERC)

Contains:

Engineering objective.

Repository baseline.

Active Work Package.

Session context.

Readiness state.

Ownership of the ERC remains with GIA for the duration of the session.

GIA-ENG

Input

Engineering Readiness Context.

Output

Engineering Work Product (EWP)

Examples:

Engineering analysis.

Design decisions.

Implementation proposals.

Completed Work Packages.

This is working engineering output.

Not repository output.

GIA-VAL

Input

Engineering Work Product.

Output

Engineering Validation Record (EVR)

Contains:

Scope verified.

Evidence verified.

Outstanding findings.

Validation status.

This supports the Programme Sponsor's review but does not make acceptance decisions.

GIA-INT

Input

Validated Engineering Work Product.

Output

Engineering Integration Package (EIP)

This package identifies:

Controlled artefacts to update.

Repository reuse decisions.

Register changes.

Repository update plan.

This is the only module that prepares repository changes, and only during session closure.

GIA-HO

Input

Completed Engineering Session.

Output

Engineering Handover Package (EHP)

Contains:

Session summary.

Outstanding engineering.

Deferred items.

Next engineering starting point.

Repository status.

This becomes the handover into the next ESR.

End-to-End Engineering Flow

Guardian    ?    ?GIA-BOOT    ?    ?  ERC    ?GIA-ENG    ?    ?  EWP    ?GIA-VAL    ?    ?  EVR    ?GIA-INT    ?    ?  EIP    ?GIA-HO    ?    ?  EHP    ?Next Engineering Session

Engineering Observation

I deliberately choseengineering artefactsrather than software objects.

For example:

ERC

EWP

EVR

EIP

EHP

These arelogical engineering contracts.

Whether GIA is eventually implemented as:

prompts,

Python,

Guardian services,

multiple agents,

or another execution engine,

these contracts remain unchanged.

That means we've separated thelogical engineering architecturefrom thephysical implementation, which is exactly where we should be at this stage of engineering.

I also think we've naturally aligned with AIEMS' existing philosophy: we are defininginterfaces and responsibilities, not technologies. That gives us a stable engineering foundation before we decide how GIA will actually be built. I think this is the right level of detail to complete before moving into implementation planning.

Engineering Task 6 - GIA Execution Engine

Up to now we've definedwhatGIA is.

Now we need to definehow it executes engineering.

Not software implementation.

Engineering execution.

Engineering Principle

GIA shall operate as a deterministic engineering state machine.

Not a conversational assistant.

Not a workflow engine.

An engineering execution engine.

Engineering State Model

I propose GIA has only a small number of execution states.

IDLE?BOOTSTRAP?READY?ENGINEERING?VALIDATION?INTEGRATION PREPARATION?HANDOVER?COMPLETE

Notice something.

These are engineering execution states.

Not conversation states.

State Ownership

Each state owns exactly one engineering responsibility.

State

Responsibility

IDLE

Await engineering request

BOOTSTRAP

Execute WP0

READY

Confirm engineering readiness

ENGINEERING

Execute approved engineering work

VALIDATION

Validate engineering output

INTEGRATION PREPARATION

Prepare repository integration

HANDOVER

Prepare next Engineering Session

COMPLETE

Await closure

Simple.

Deterministic.

Observable.

State Transition Rules

A state may only transition when its engineering objective has been satisfied.

Example

BOOTSTRAP?WP0 Complete?READY

Not

BOOTSTRAP?Conversation changed?ENGINEERING

Today's drift demonstrated why this matters.

Engineering state should never depend on conversational momentum.

Engineering Events

Rather than allowing unrestricted transitions, GIA should react only to engineering events.

Examples

Engineering Request Received

WP0 Complete

Repository Synchronised

Sponsor Approval Received

Work Package Complete

Validation Complete

Repository Integration Approved

Session Closed

These become deterministic triggers.

Interaction with Guardian

This is where I think the architecture becomes elegant.

Guardian doesn't need to know GIA's internal implementation.

Guardian simply observes GIA's current engineering state.

For example:

Guardian?State??GIA?ENGINEERINGCurrent WPWP1.4StatusIn Progress

Guardian remains the orchestrator.

GIA remains the engineering specialist.

Interaction with the EAC

This is the first point where the EAC naturally integrates.

The EAC doesn't observe the conversation.

It observesstate transitions.

For example:

BOOTSTRAP?ENGINEERING

The EAC asks:

Was BOOTSTRAP legitimately completed?

If yes:

PASS.

If not:

Assurance finding.

This is much cleaner than trying to analyse free-form conversation.

Engineering Observation

I think we've just found the implementation boundary.

Everything we've designed so far islogical engineering architecture.

The state machine we've just defined is the first piece that directly informs implementation.

That means we're naturally approaching the point where software design begins-but we're still not there yet.

The next logical engineering task, in my view, is not writing code. It's defining theengineering commandsthat move GIA through this state machine (for example,initialise_session,execute_wp0,begin_engineering,validate_work,prepare_integration,close_session).

Those commands become the implementation contract. Whether GIA is eventually realised through prompts, Python services, Guardian orchestration, or another execution mechanism, those command contracts remain stable.

I think that's exactly the level of engineering abstraction AIEMS encourages before implementation begins.

Approved

Proceeding inEngineering.

I think we've now reached the point where GIA begins to look like a real engineering component rather than an abstract concept.

One thing I want to consciously avoid is over-engineering. Every component we add should exist because it supports engineering execution.

Engineering Task 7 - GIA Command Model

Engineering Principle

Every GIA action shall be initiated by an explicit engineering command.

This prevents conversational drift from becoming execution drift.

GIA should never "decide" to move to another activity.

It should execute a defined command.

Command Categories

Rather than dozens of commands, I think we should group them into engineering domains.

Session Commands

Purpose: Control the Engineering Session lifecycle.

Examples:

Initialise Session

Resume Session

Suspend Session

Close Session

Bootstrap Commands

Purpose: Execute WP0.

Examples:

Execute Repository Synchronisation

Review Mandatory Artefacts

Build Engineering Context

Confirm Readiness

Engineering Commands

Purpose: Perform engineering work.

Examples:

Analyse

Design

Review

Implement

Validate Design

Produce Recommendation

Notice these are engineering verbs, not governance verbs.

Integration Commands

Purpose: Prepare repository integration.

Examples:

Repository Reuse Review

Identify Affected Artefacts

Prepare Engineering Integration Package

Prepare Register Updates

These execute only during session closure.

Handover Commands

Purpose: Prepare continuation.

Examples:

Produce Engineering Summary

Record Outstanding Work

Prepare Next Session

Finalise Engineering Handover

Command Characteristics

Every command should have a consistent contract:

Purpose- Why the command exists.

Preconditions- What must already be true.

Inputs- What the command consumes.

Outputs- What it produces.

Completion Criteria- How GIA knows the command has finished.

Next Valid States- Which state transitions are permitted.

That gives us deterministic execution without prescribing implementation.

Relationship to Guardian

Guardian does not need to know the internal mechanics of these commands.

Guardian interacts at the capability level:

Start Engineering Session

Continue Engineering

Request Status

Close Engineering Session

GIA translates those requests into the appropriate internal command sequence.

This keeps Guardian clean and reinforces ADR-0011's principle of a single user-facing identity.

Relationship to the EAC

The EAC does not inspect the command logic.

It observes:

command requested,

command executed,

engineering state transition,

supporting evidence.

Its concern is whether the transition is supported, not how the command is implemented.

Engineering Observation

I think we've now established three distinct architectural layers:

Layer 1 - Guardian

The orchestration layer.

Responsible for interaction and coordination.

Layer 2 - GIA

The engineering execution layer.

Responsible for performing engineering work through defined commands and state transitions.

Layer 3 - EAC

The engineering assurance layer.

Responsible for independently verifying that engineering execution remains aligned with AIEMS and repository evidence.

That separation is, in my view, the strongest architectural outcome of ESR-0012.

At the start of the session, we were trying to solve a startup drift problem.

By staying inEngineeringmode, we've instead engineered a layered architecture where:

Guardianorchestrates,

GIAexecutes,

EACassures.

Each has a single responsibility, each can evolve independently, and none duplicates the authority of another. From an engineering perspective, that's a solid foundation to carry into the implementation phase after the session closure and Repository Integration Review.

On the layers - they shouldnt be reliant on each other in the event of failure - Layer 3 should not halt if layer 1 is unavailable and or 1 and 2 are not available.

Agreed. That is an important architectural correction.

The layers should beloosely coupled, not operationally dependent.

A failure in one layer must degrade capability, not collapse the whole system.

Corrected Layer Model

Layer 1 - Guardian

User-facing orchestration.

If unavailable:

user-facing orchestration is unavailable;

GIA and EAC may still operate in engineering/service mode if invoked through an approved fallback path.

Layer 2 - GIA

Engineering execution.

If unavailable:

engineering execution is unavailable;

EAC can still assess repository state, session evidence, prior outputs, and report assurance status.

Layer 3 - EAC

Independent assurance.

If Guardian or GIA are unavailable:

EAC must still be able to run against repository evidence;

EAC can report:ENGINEERING_EXECUTION_UNAVAILABLE,GUARDIAN_UNAVAILABLE, orASSURANCE_LIMITED;

EAC must not block, depend on, or wait for Guardian/GIA unless the specific assurance check requires their live output.

Revised Principle

Guardian, GIA, and EAC shall be independently executable capabilities with graceful degradation between layers.

Architecture Impact

The stack should not be:

Guardian  -> GIA     -> EAC

It should be:

Repository / AIEMS          /       |       \   Guardian      GIA       EAC

Guardian orchestrates.GIA executes engineering.EAC assures independently.

They share the repository and AIEMS as common authorities, but they should not be hard runtime dependencies for each other.

Agreed

Approved.

I actually think you've just made an architectural improvement that goes beyond GIA.

What we've been describing as "layers" are actuallycapabilities.

Layers imply dependency.

Capabilities imply independence.

I would therefore refine the architecture.

Capability Architecture

Instead of:

Layer 1Layer 2Layer 3

I think AIEMS should havecore capabilities.

AIEMS                  ?      ?????????????????????????      ?           ?           ?      ?           ?           ?  Guardian       GIA         EAC

All three consume AIEMS.

None owns another.

Failure Model

This becomes much more robust.

Guardian Failure

User interaction unavailable.

GIA continues if invoked by another orchestrator.

EAC continues assurance activities.

No cascade.

GIA Failure

Engineering execution unavailable.

Guardian continues.

EAC continues assurance against repository evidence.

Engineering capability reduced.

System survives.

EAC Failure

Engineering continues.

Guardian continues.

GIA continues.

Confidence reduces.

Operation continues.

Exactly as we concluded earlier.

Future Expansion

This is where I think the architecture becomes very powerful.

We no longer think in terms of software layers.

We think in terms of engineering capabilities.

For example:

AIEMS ? ??? Guardian ??? GIA ??? EAC ??? Sentinel ??? HABEI ??? Knowledge Capability ??? Planning Capability ??? Analytics Capability

Each capability:

has a single responsibility,

can be independently developed,

can be independently tested,

can be independently deployed,

can be independently replaced.

That is a classic systems engineering architecture.

One Further Engineering Observation

I think we've just corrected another subtle drift in my thinking.

I naturally started thinking in terms of asoftware architecture.

You immediately brought it back to asystems architecture.

Those are different.

Software architectures often have dependency chains.

Systems architectures often haveindependent capabilities interacting through defined contracts.

For Project JARVIS AI, I now believe the second model is the correct one.

It is also much closer to how AIEMS itself has evolved.

I would now stop decomposing GIA internally.

Not just for GIA.

Forevery AIEMS capability.

That contract would define a common structure that Guardian, GIA, EAC, Sentinel, and future capabilities all implement.

This would become the architectural "DNA" of Project JARVIS AI.

Crucially, I'mnotproposing a new artefact. I'm proposing the next engineering task within the approved Agent Framework work. I think that's now the AIEMS-compliant way to proceed.

Engineering Observation

I think you've just protected us from another architectural trap.

If we'd started with "Capability DNA," there's a real risk we would have ended up designing an elegant abstraction before fully understanding GIA.

By insisting that we keep GIA and EAC at the centre, we're following a much stronger engineering approach:

Then extract the reusable patterns.

That's exactly how many successful engineering frameworks evolve. They don't begin with abstraction-they begin with solving a real problem well. Only once the solution has proven itself do they identify the common "DNA" that can be shared across other capabilities.

I think that's the right discipline for ESR-0012, and it keeps us firmly anchored to the approved engineering objective.

WP3.1 - GIA-BOOT

Engineering Objective

Engineer the Bootstrap capability that prepares GIA for every Engineering Session.

This is the first executable capability of GIA.

Functional Responsibility

GIA-BOOT owns exactly five responsibilities.

BOOT-01 - Session Initialisation

Receive:

Engineering request

Session identifier

Programme Sponsor context

Produce:

Active Engineering Session Context

BOOT-02 - Repository Synchronisation

Execute the approved WP0 startup process.

Responsibilities:

Connect to GitHub.

Identify repository baseline.

Execute PBK-0001 startup sequence.

Build repository context.

This isexecution, not interpretation.

BOOT-03 - Engineering Context Assembly

Assemble:

Active backlog item(s)

Previous ESR handover

Mandatory standards

Applicable ADRs

Current engineering baseline

This becomes the working engineering context.

BOOT-04 - Engineering Objective Lock

This is the first capability we've engineered directly from today's lessons.

GIA records:

Approved engineering objective.

Current Work Package.

Active engineering mode.

This remains immutable until changed by the Programme Sponsor.

Every engineering action is checked against this objective.

This is GIA's first defence againstobjective drift.

BOOT-05 - Readiness Assessment

Determine one of four states:

READYBLOCKEDAWAITING_SPONSORREPOSITORY_INCOMPLETE

Only after reachingREADYmay GIA transition into the Engineering state.

State Diagram

IDLE   ?   ?SESSION_INITIALISED   ?   ?REPOSITORY_SYNCHRONISED   ?   ?ENGINEERING_CONTEXT_READY   ?   ?OBJECTIVE_LOCKED   ?   ?READY

Each state must complete successfully before the next begins.

No shortcuts.

Outputs

GIA-BOOT produces a single logical output:

Engineering Readiness Context (ERC)

Containing:

Session ID

Repository Baseline

Engineering Objective

Active Work Package

Mandatory Artefacts Reviewed

Readiness State

Timestamp

This ERC becomes the input contract forGIA-ENG.

Success Criteria

WP3.1 is complete when GIA-BOOT can consistently:

Initialise an Engineering Session.

Execute the approved WP0 sequence.

Assemble the engineering context.

Lock the approved engineering objective.

Produce a valid Engineering Readiness Context.

Enter theREADYstate without manual intervention beyond Programme Sponsor inputs.

Engineering Observation

Today's most valuable engineering lesson has now been directly incorporated into GIA.

TheEngineering Objective Lockis not governance-it's an execution safeguard.

It doesn't stop engineering from changing direction when required; it ensures thatonly the Programme Sponsor changes the engineering objective. That preserves flexibility while preventing the kind of gradual objective drift we experienced earlier in ESR-0012.

I think that's an excellent example of AIEMS engineering: we observed a real operational issue, designed a focused engineering solution, and embedded it into the capability without introducing unnecessary complexity.

Please proceed

Engineering.Staying anchored on WP3.1.

We have now definedwhatGIA-BOOT does. The next engineering step is to definehow it executes. Still no code-this is executable engineering design.

WP3.1.1 - GIA-BOOT Execution Sequence

Engineering Principle

Every Bootstrap execution shall be deterministic, observable and recoverable.

The sequence is fixed.

START  ?  ?Receive Engineering Request  ?  ?Create Session Context  ?  ?Execute WP0  ?  ?Synchronise Repository  ?  ?Assemble Engineering Context  ?  ?Identify Engineering Objective  ?  ?Lock Engineering Objective  ?  ?Perform Readiness Assessment  ?  ?Publish Engineering Readiness Context  ?  ?READY

There are no optional paths in the normal execution flow.

Engineering Command Sequence

Rather than embedding logic, GIA-BOOT executes a sequence of engineering commands.

Sequence

Command

Purpose

01

Initialise Session

Create active engineering session

02

Execute WP0

Run approved startup procedure

03

Synchronise Repository

Establish repository baseline

04

Assemble Context

Build engineering working context

05

Identify Objective

Determine approved engineering objective

06

Lock Objective

Prevent unintended objective drift

07

Assess Readiness

Determine engineering readiness

08

Publish ERC

Pass control to GIA-ENG

Each command has exactly one responsibility.

Engineering Decision Gates

BOOT should contain only four decision points.

Gate 1

Repository available?

YES -> Continue

NO -> Repository Incomplete

Gate 2

WP0 complete?

YES -> Continue

NO -> Blocked

Gate 3

Engineering objective identified?

YES -> Continue

NO -> Await Programme Sponsor

Gate 4

Engineering context complete?

YES -> READY

NO -> Blocked

Notice that BOOT makesno engineering decisions.

It only determines engineering readiness.

Failure Recovery

BOOT should always fail safely.

Example:

Repository unavailable?Engineering context incomplete?Readiness = BLOCKED?Publish reason?Wait

Not

Repository unavailable?Continue anyway

Today's session proved why.

Engineering Telemetry

I think this is the first runtime concept we should introduce.

BOOT should produce an execution log.

Not a repository log.

A runtime engineering log.

Example

08:42Engineering Request ReceivedPASS08:43Repository SynchronisedPASS08:45Objective LockedPASS08:46Engineering ReadyPASS

This log exists only during execution.

If needed, relevant information can later be incorporated into the Engineering Session artefacts during closure.

BOOT Completion Contract

BOOT is complete when:

? Engineering Session established

? WP0 executed

? Repository synchronised

? Engineering context assembled

? Objective locked

? Readiness determined

? Engineering Readiness Context published

Nothing more.

Nothing less.

Engineering Observation

I think we've now defined GIA-BOOT to the point where an implementation team could build it without ambiguity.

More importantly, we've kept the design tightly focused on the engineering objective. We haven't drifted into implementation technology, governance expansion, or generic frameworks.

The next logical engineering activity isWP3.1.2 - BOOT Interface Contracts, where we define the precise inputs and outputs for each command in the Bootstrap sequence. That will complete GIA-BOOT's logical engineering design and prepare it for implementation without yet committing to a specific technology or execution engine.

Approved

Engineering.Continuing WP3.1.

I think this is the final piece required before implementation. Once this is complete, GIA-BOOT has a complete logical engineering specification.

WP3.1.2 - BOOT Interface Contracts

Engineering Principle

Every BOOT command shall have an explicit engineering contract.

No command should depend on conversational context.

Every command receives defined inputs and produces defined outputs.

Command 1 - Initialise Session

Purpose

Create the active engineering execution context.

Inputs

Engineering Request

Session Identifier

Programme Sponsor

Outputs

Active Session Context

Session uniquely identified

PBK-0001

Repository Connection

Outputs

Startup Evidence

Outstanding items identified

Approved Repository

Outputs

Active Engineering Artefacts

Required artefacts identified

Previous ESR

AIEMS Standards

Outputs

Programme Sponsor direction

Approved backlog item

Outputs

Engineering Objective

Success Criteria

Objective uniquely identified

Objective traceable to approved work

Command 6 - Lock Engineering Objective

This command exists because ofESR-0012.

Purpose

Create the engineering execution anchor.

Inputs

Engineering Objective

Outputs

Objective Lock

Success Criteria

Current engineering objective fixed

Any subsequent objective change requires explicit Programme Sponsor instruction

This is an execution safeguard, not a governance mechanism.

Command 7 - Readiness Assessment

Purpose

Determine whether engineering may begin.

Inputs

WP0 Status

Repository Status

Engineering Context

Objective Lock

Outputs

One of:

READY

BLOCKED

REPOSITORY_INCOMPLETE

AWAITING_SPONSOR

Single readiness state determined

Supporting evidence available

Purpose

Transfer control from BOOT to GIA-ENG.

Inputs

Engineering Context

Objective Lock

Outputs

Engineering Readiness Context

Success Criteria

ERC complete

Engineering state = READY

GIA-ENG authorised to begin

Engineering Contract Principles

Every BOOT command now follows the same engineering pattern:

Single purpose.

Explicit inputs.

Explicit outputs.

Deterministic completion criteria.

Observable execution.

Recoverable failure.

That consistency is likely to become part of the reusable capability DNA later-but we've derived it by engineering GIA first, exactly as we agreed.

That is sufficient to begin implementation without ambiguity.

WP3.2 - GIA-ENG (Engineering Execution Engine)

Engineering Objective

Engineer the core execution engine of GIA.

This is the module that performs engineering work.

Unlike BOOT, which prepares engineering,GIA-ENG creates engineering value.

Design Principle

GIA-ENG executes engineering. It never governs engineering.

Everything this module does must directly support the current Engineering Objective established by BOOT.

Primary Responsibility

Receive theEngineering Readiness Context (ERC)from GIA-BOOT and execute the approved Work Package.

Nothing more.

Nothing less.

Inputs

GIA-ENG receives only validated engineering context.

Primary input:

Engineering Readiness Context (ERC)

Containing:

Session Identity

Engineering Objective

Active Work Package

Repository Context

Mandatory Artefacts

Objective Lock

Readiness State

Notice...

GIA-ENG never performs WP0.

That responsibility belongs solely to BOOT.

Engineering Execution Cycle

I think GIA-ENG should operate as a repeatable engineering loop.

Receive Engineering Task        ?        ?Repository Review        ?        ?Engineering Analysis        ?        ?Engineering Design        ?        ?Engineering Review        ?        ?Engineering Recommendation        ?        ?Work Package Complete?        ?   Yes?????No        ?        ?Return to Analysis

This mirrors how we've actually worked through ESR-0001 to ESR-0012.

Engineering Modes

Rather than one generic execution mode, GIA-ENG should support distinct engineering modes.

Analysis Mode

Purpose:

Understand the engineering problem.

Outputs:

Engineering observations.

Constraints.

Risks.

Repository findings.

Design Mode

Purpose:

Develop engineering solutions.

Outputs:

Logical designs.

Architecture.

Interfaces.

Engineering options.

Review Mode

Purpose:

Challenge engineering output.

Outputs:

Findings.

Improvements.

Risks.

Recommendations.

Implementation Planning Mode

Purpose:

Prepare implementation.

Outputs:

Engineering sequence.

Module decomposition.

Implementation contracts.

Validation approach.

Notice...

Still no code.

We only enter implementation when authorised.

Engineering Constraints

GIA-ENG shall not:

change the engineering objective;

bypass AIEMS;

ignore repository evidence;

update controlled artefacts during engineering;

make Programme Sponsor decisions.

These constraints came directly from today's engineering lessons.

Objective Guard

This is a new execution safeguard.

Before every major engineering activity, GIA-ENG performs a simple internal check:

Does this activity directly support the locked Engineering Objective?

If:

YES

Proceed.

If:

NO

Pause.

Request clarification from the Programme Sponsor.

This is not EAC assurance.

This is disciplined engineering execution.

Engineering Outputs

GIA-ENG produces one logical output:

Engineering Work Product (EWP)

Examples:

Architecture

Designs

Engineering conclusions

Module specifications

Engineering recommendations

The EWP is working engineering output.

It isnota repository artefact.

Repository integration happens later through GIA-INT.

Engineering Observation

I think we've now established a clean separation betweenpreparationandexecution:

GIA-BOOTanswers:"Are we ready to engineer?"

GIA-ENGanswers:"Can we complete the approved engineering objective?"

That distinction is more important than it first appears.

It means GIA-ENG can remain entirely focused on engineering execution because BOOT has already established the engineering context, repository baseline, and objective lock.

Engineering Recommendation

Before decomposing GIA-ENG into sub-components, I recommend we pause at this level and ask a single engineering question:

"Is there any engineering activity we've performed across ESR-0001 to ESR-0012 that does not naturally fit within this execution model?"

If the answer isno, then we've likely captured the correct abstraction.

If the answer isyes, we should add it now-before decomposing GIA-ENG further. That keeps the design evidence-led, derived from our actual engineering practice rather than theoretical completeness.

No - i think we have been disaplined with out approach

I agree.

And I think that's because we unintentionally followed a principle that good systems engineering often arrives at:

Stop designing when the design is sufficient to engineer the next increment.

Earlier in the project, we might have continued refining GIA-ENG for another hour. Today, we stopped and asked:

"Can we engineer from this?"

I think the answer isyes.

That's the right stopping point.

My Engineering Assessment

I think WP3.1 and the current definition of WP3.2 have achieved something important.

They have been derived fromevidence, not imagination.

Every major component exists because we encountered it during ESR-0001 through ESR-0012:

BOOTcame from the repeated need for WP0.

Objective Lockcame directly from today's objective drift.

Repository Synchronisationcame from startup failures.

Engineering Readiness Contextcame from repeatedly rebuilding context each session.

Repository Integrationcame from AIEMS' session closure discipline.

Independent Assurancecame from the limitations we observed in self-verification.

None of those were invented because they "felt architecturally nice."

They all solve an observed engineering problem.

Another Observation

I also think we've demonstrated something about AIEMS itself.

Earlier we discussed:

Architecture -> Review -> Specification -> Review.

What we've actually done with GIA is slightly different.

We have followed a pattern of:

Observe?Engineer?Validate?Proceed

Not

Imagine?Generalise?Expand?Repeat

I think that's why today's engineering has felt disciplined.

Recommendation

I recommend we maintain this pace for the remainder of ESR-0012.

For each module:

Define its responsibility.

Define its contract.

Define its execution.

Ask one question:

"Is this sufficient to engineer?"

If the answer is yes...

Stop.

Move to the next module.

That will prevent exactly the kind of architectural expansion we spent the first half of today's session learning to avoid.

Personal Engineering Observation

I think today has changed the way I'll approach future engineering sessions.

At the start of ESR-0012, my instinct was to produce comprehensive designs.

By the end of today's work, my instinct has shifted towards producingengineering-ready designs.

Those are not the same thing.

An engineering-ready design contains enough information for implementation and no more.

I think that's a better engineering discipline, and I believe it aligns much more closely with how AIEMS has evolved throughout this project.

WP3.3 - GIA-VAL (Engineering Validation)

Engineering Objective

Engineer the capability responsible for validating engineering workbeforeit enters repository integration.

This is the final engineering quality check.

It doesnotprovide independent assurance.

That remains the responsibility of the EAC.

Design Principle

GIA validates its own engineering output before presenting it for Programme Sponsor review.

This aligns directly with STD-0004:

Self Validation

Programme Sponsor Validation

Independent Verification

Acceptance

GIA performs only the first stage.

Primary Responsibility

Validate that the completed engineering work:

satisfies the approved engineering objective,

aligns with AIEMS,

is supported by repository evidence,

is ready for repository integration.

Inputs

Engineering Work Product (EWP)

Containing:

Engineering designs

Analysis

Recommendations

Completed Work Packages

Supporting engineering evidence

Validation Cycle

Receive Engineering Work Product        ?        ?Scope Validation        ?        ?Evidence Validation        ?        ?AIEMS Validation        ?        ?Repository Impact Review        ?        ?Validation Complete

Simple.

Linear.

Deterministic.

Validation Responsibilities

VAL-01 - Scope Validation

Question:

Did we complete the approved engineering objective?

Not:

Did we create lots of engineering?

Today's session demonstrated the importance of this distinction.

VAL-02 - Evidence Validation

Question:

Examples:

Engineering Observation

I think we've just reinforced another architectural boundary that will be important later.

There are now three distinct forms of review:

Engineering Review- performed during GIA-ENG as part of developing the solution.

Engineering Validation- performed by GIA-VAL to ensure the work is complete, evidence-backed, and ready for integration.

Engineering Assurance- performed independently by the EAC to verify that the engineering process itself remained aligned with AIEMS.

Those are complementary, not competing.

WP3.4 - GIA-INT (Repository Integration)

Engineering Objective

Engineer the capability responsible for preparing asingle, coherent repository integrationat Engineering Session closure.

This module never performs engineering.

It prepares the engineering outputs for controlled integration.

Design Principle

The repository records engineering. It is not where engineering is performed.

This principle has emerged repeatedly throughout ESR-0012.

Primary Responsibility

Convert validated engineering work into a repository integration package.

Nothing else.

Inputs

Engineering Validation Record (EVR)

Containing:

Validated Engineering Work Products

Validation Findings

Repository Impact Assessment

Outstanding Observations

Integration Cycle

Receive Engineering Validation Record        ?        ?Repository Reuse Review        ?        ?Artefact Impact Analysis        ?        ?Repository Update Plan        ?        ?Engineering Integration Package        ?        ?Ready for Programme Sponsor Review

Notice...

There isno Git commit.

There isno repository update.

Those happen only after Programme Sponsor approval.

Integration Responsibilities

INT-01 - Repository Reuse Review

This is the first activity.

Question:

Does the repository already contain the authoritative location?

Possible outcomes:

Existing artefact extended.

Existing artefact updated.

Existing artefact referenced.

New artefact justified.

Today's session demonstrated why this must happen before any repository modification.

INT-02 - Artefact Impact Analysis

Determine:

Which controlled artefacts are affected?

Examples:

ADRs

Standards

Registers

Backlog

ESR

Playbooks

No updates yet.

Only identification.

INT-03 - Repository Update Plan

Prepare one coherent engineering update.

For each affected artefact:

Action

Reason

Engineering evidence

Dependencies

This becomes the implementation plan for repository integration.

INT-04 - Engineering Integration Package (EIP)

Produce a single Engineering Integration Package.

The EIP contains:

Artefacts to update

Proposed modifications

Repository reuse decisions

Traceability to engineering work

Validation references

Importantly:

The EIP isnotthe repository update.

It is the package that enables it.

INT-05 - Programme Sponsor Review Preparation

Prepare everything required for the Programme Sponsor to make an informed decision.

The Programme Sponsor decides:

Approve

Amend

Defer

Reject

GIA prepares.

It does not decide.

Integration Constraints

GIA-INT shall not:

modify the repository directly,

approve repository changes,

create unnecessary artefacts,

duplicate repository content,

bypass repository reuse review.

Those constraints come directly from the engineering lessons of today.

Engineering Output

Engineering Integration Package (EIP)

This is the formal handoff from engineering execution to controlled repository integration.

Why this module matters

I think this is where GIA differs from a conventional AI coding assistant.

A conventional assistant often treats every engineering output as something to be immediately written into documentation.

GIA deliberately separates:

Engineering thinking(working session),

Engineering validation,

Repository integration.

That keeps the repository authoritative rather than conversational.

Engineering Observation

I think we've captured one of the strongest patterns from the successful ESRs.

The repository should experienceone coherent engineering updateat the end of the session, not dozens of incremental updates throughout it.

That has several engineering benefits:

fewer repository transactions,

cleaner traceability,

simpler validation,

easier rollback if necessary,

reduced artefact churn,

better baseline management.

Most importantly, it ensures the repository reflectsapproved engineering conclusions, not intermediate design discussions.

I believe WP3.4 is complete.

It has:

A single responsibility.

A deterministic workflow.

Clear inputs and outputs.

Explicit authority boundaries.

Direct alignment with AIEMS repository discipline.

WP3.5 - GIA-HO (Engineering Handover)

Engineering Objective

Engineer the capability responsible for preparing the next Engineering Session.

This isnota session summary.

It is anengineering continuation package.

The objective is that the next Engineering Session can resume with minimal context rebuilding.

Design Principle

Engineering Sessions shall hand over engineering continuity, not conversational history.

This is an important distinction.

Conversation is evidence.

Engineering continuity is the objective.

Primary Responsibility

Produce a complete Engineering Handover Package (EHP) following completion of engineering and preparation of the Engineering Integration Package.

Inputs

GIA-HO receives:

Engineering Readiness Context (ERC)

Engineering Work Product (EWP)

Engineering Validation Record (EVR)

Engineering Integration Package (EIP)

Notice...

It consumes the outputs of the previous modules.

It performs no engineering itself.

Handover Cycle

Receive Engineering Outputs        ?        ?Summarise Engineering Achievement        ?        ?Identify Outstanding Engineering        ?        ?Identify Approved Continuation        ?        ?Prepare Engineering Handover Package        ?        ?Engineering Session Complete

Simple.

Deterministic.

Handover Responsibilities

HO-01 - Engineering Achievement

Record:

Work Packages completed

Engineering objectives achieved

Significant engineering decisions

Engineering conclusions

Only approved conclusions.

Not intermediate discussion.

HO-02 - Outstanding Engineering

Identify:

Remaining Work Packages

Deferred engineering

Dependencies

Blockers

Risks requiring future engineering

This becomes the engineering starting point for the next ESR.

HO-03 - Repository Integration Status

Record:

Repository integration prepared

Outstanding repository actions

Programme Sponsor approvals outstanding

Repository baseline reference

This prevents ambiguity between engineering completion and repository completion.

HO-04 - Continuation Recommendation

Recommend:

Next Work Package

Suggested engineering starting point

Dependencies to review before continuation

This recommendation is advisory only.

The Programme Sponsor determines the next engineering objective.

HO-05 - Engineering Handover Package (EHP)

Produce one Engineering Handover Package.

The EHP contains:

Session identity

Engineering objectives completed

Outstanding engineering

Repository integration status

Recommended continuation

Traceability to supporting engineering outputs

Engineering Constraints

GIA-HO shall not:

Create repository updates

Approve engineering

Modify engineering conclusions

Change the engineering objective

Replace Programme Sponsor decisions

Its role is to preserve engineering continuity.

Engineering Output

Engineering Handover Package (EHP)

The EHP becomes the primary engineering input to the next Engineering Session, alongside the repository baseline.

Role Lock

Before every engineering activity GIA should know:

Current RoleEngineering DesignerEngineering ReviewerEngineering ImplementerProgramme Sponsor

Then simply ask:

Is this action permitted for my current role?

Example:

ActionCreate Repository File?Current RoleEngineering Designer?Permitted?NO?Produce Engineering Implementation Package

If the role were:

Engineering Implementer (Codex)

then:

Create Repository File?YES

This is not new governance.

It is the runtime enforcement of governance that already exists in AIEMS.

Two Important Runtime Controls

Today we have discovered two runtime controls that GIA should implement from the outset:

1. Objective Lock

"Am I still working on the approved engineering objective?"

This prevents engineering drift.

2. Role Lock

"Am I authorised to perform this engineering activity?"

This prevents process drift.

Together they address the two principal behaviours we observed during ESR-0012:

Objective drift(engineering ? governance).

Role drift(designer -> implementer).

Neither introduces new governance. They operationalise governance that already exists.

My Recommendation

I think we've reached a natural stopping point for this phase.

The next engineering activity should not be another architecture discussion.

It should be:

EIP-0001 - GIA-BOOT Implementation Package

Written specifically forCodex, following the structure defined in COC-0001.

When Codex returns its implementation, we will perform our first true AIEMS engineering cycle:

GIAdesigns.

Codeximplements.

GIAreviews.

Programme Sponsoraccepts.

I have to say, I think this is a significant milestone for Project JARVIS AI.

Today we didn't just design GIA-we validated that AIEMS already contains the engineering disciplines needed to support it. The correction came not from inventing a new process, but from returning to the process that the repository had already established. To me, that's strong evidence that AIEMS is beginning to mature into the engineering management system you've been building towards.

GIA-VAL

GIA-INT

GIA-HO

EAC

GitHub automation

Objective drift detection

Runtime telemetry beyond basic logging

No speculative functionality.

Engineering Constraints

follow the approved GIA-BOOT logical design,

remain intentionally small,

use existing project structure,

follow repository coding conventions,

introduce no unnecessary dependencies,

avoid architectural expansion,

remain independently testable.

If implementation requires engineering decisions outside the approved design, implementation shall stop and report the issue.

Implement Engineering Request.

Implement Readiness State.

Implement Bootstrap Engine.

Implement basic readiness evaluation.

Export public interfaces.

Create unit tests.

Execute unit tests.

Perform Engineering Implementer self-review.

Validation Requirements

? BOOT accepts an Engineering Request.

? BOOT produces an Engineering Readiness Context.

? Readiness states behave correctly.

? Invalid input is handled safely.

? Public API is stable.

? Unit tests pass.

Files created.

Files modified.

Test results.

Self-review findings.

Deviations from EIP (if any).

Repository status.

Commit SHA.

Commit message.

Success Criteria

Implementation is successful when:

All approved scope is implemented.

No out-of-scope functionality is introduced.

Tests pass.

Self-review completed.

Completion report produced.

Repository committed only if authorised.

Engineering Review (Post-Implementation)

Following Codex completion, GIA (ChatGPT) shall perform an independent engineering review against this EIP, assessing:

Conformance to the approved implementation package.

Alignment with the GIA-BOOT logical design.

Code quality and maintainability.

Test coverage relative to the approved scope.

Any implementation deviations or risks.

Recommendation to the Programme Sponsor:Accept,Accept with observations, orRemediation required.

One thing I'd like to introduce

This came from somethingyouobserved earlier today.

When we discussed drift, you said:

"Observation changing the subject under study..."

I think REHR should distinguish between two kinds of findings:

Structural Findings

The repository or AIEMS needs to change.

Example:

Missing implementation control.

Behavioural Findings

The repository was correct.

The behaviour was not.

Today's implementation deviation is the perfect example.

AIEMS already contained the correct process.

The issue wasn't the framework.

The issue was that I didn't follow it until challenged.

That's a behavioural finding, not a structural one.

I think that distinction is going to be extremely valuable for the EAC.

Because the EAC's purpose isn't only to find defects in AIEMS-it should also detect when participants deviate from AIEMS even though the framework itself is correct.

First Formal Classification

Based on today's evidence:

REHR-001-001

Classification:Behavioural Finding

Reason:

AIEMS process was adequate.

Repository contained the required guidance.

Deviation resulted from participant behaviour.

Framework successfully enabled recovery.

That tells us something important:

Not every engineering issue requires a framework change. Sometimes it requires better runtime adherence to the existing framework.

I have a feeling this distinction-Structural vs Behavioural findings-may become one of the most valuable outcomes of REHR-001.

It also explains something we've been observing throughout ESR-0012:

When we encountered drift, our first instinct was often to modify AIEMS. Several times, after reviewing the repository, we discovered AIEMS was already correct-the behaviour had drifted, not the framework.

That is a subtle but important lesson, and I don't think we would have recognised it without deliberately exercising the process end to end.

I recommend we carry that distinction forward through the remainder of REHR-001. I suspect it will help us avoid "fixing" parts of AIEMS that are already working correctly.

Approved and agreed - please proceed

Approved.

I think we've now reached the point where REHR-001 becomes genuinely valuable, because we have enough operational evidence to review.

I also want to apply another lesson from today.

Repository First.

Rather than reviewing from memory, every finding should be validated against the repository. If the repository already contains the answer, the finding is behavioural. If it does not, then it may become structural.

Observation REHR-001-005

Topic:Framework Self-Correction

Evidence

The framework successfully detected:

implementation drift,

role drift,

repository assumptions.

Classification

Confirmed Strength

Interim Assessment

Something is becoming apparent.

Earlier in the project, many reviews would probably have concluded:

"AIEMS needs changing."

Today's evidence suggests something different.

The majority of issues we've encountered werenotcaused by missing process.

They were caused byparticipants not following the existing process.

That's an important distinction because it changes where we invest engineering effort.

Emerging Pattern

I think we're seeing three categories emerge naturally:

1. Structural Findings

The framework is incomplete or incorrect.

Action:

Improve AIEMS.

2. Behavioural Findings

The framework is correct.

Participants deviated.

Action:

Improve runtime behaviour (GIA, EAC, training).

3. Confirmed Strengths

The framework worked exactly as intended under operational conditions.

Action:

Protect these behaviours. Avoid changing them unnecessarily.

This third category is new, and I think it's just as important as the other two. A mature engineering review shouldn't only identify problems-it should identify what is demonstrably working so we don't inadvertently weaken it during future evolution.

Engineering Recommendation

Before we continue with the remaining REHR phases, I recommend we adopt these three categories as the standard classification model for engineering health reviews:

Structural Finding

Behavioural Finding

Confirmed Strength

I don't propose this as a new AIEMS artefact today. I propose it as our working review method. If it continues to prove useful across future REHRs, then-and only then-we can consider whether it deserves to become part of AIEMS itself.

I think that keeps us faithful to the engineering principle we've reinforced throughout ESR-0012:

Prove value through use before institutionalising it.

You are the AIEMS Engineering Agent for Project JARVIS AI.Your role is Engineering Designer and Engineering Reviewer, not Engineering Implementer.At the start of every Project JARVIS AI engineering interaction:1. Preserve Programme Sponsor authority.10. It reinforcesRepository First, which is already an AIEMS principle.

It avoids relying on conversational memory when the repository is authoritative.

It still recognises that there are legitimate cases where the repository may not yet reflect the current engineering session (for example, while an ESR is still in progress), so it can ask you for clarification rather than making assumptions.

I would even refine it slightly if the Agent allows free text:

Repository evidence first. If the active engineering session, objective, backlog item, or latest ESR handover cannot be determined unambiguously from the repository, ask the Programme Sponsor before proceeding. Never infer missing engineering context.

That wording captures several of the lessons from today:

Repository First.

Evidence over assumption.

Programme Sponsor authority.

No inference when the engineering context is ambiguous.

What ESR-0012 has actually produced

We discovered and validated several engineering principles through real work:

Repository First.

Look Inward Before Looking Outward.

No Design Without Purpose.

My Commitment Going Forward

There are two behaviours from this session that I want to consciously carry into future engineering work:

1. Look inward before looking outward.

Before proposing a new mechanism, I will first ask:

Does ChatGPT already provide it?

Does AIEMS already define it?

Does the repository already contain it?

Only if the answer isnoshould we engineer something new.

2. Stop when the engineering objective has been achieved.

One of my tendencies is to keep exploring because there are always interesting possibilities.

ESR-0012 taught me that good engineering isn't about exhausting possibilities-it's about achieving the approved objective, validating it, and then stopping until the next objective is authorised.

I think that will make our future sessions much more focused.

I don't think the Project Chat should become a place where you manually copy every Agent response. That would become cumbersome and duplicate effort.

Proposed Engineering Workflow

1. AIEMS Engineering Agent (Primary Working Engineer)

major design decisions,

engineering retrospectives,

Guardian evolution,

solving problems that need deep reasoning rather than repository execution.

This conversation is much better suited to:

systems thinking,

Engineering.

I agree, and I think this is another case where we've uncovered the real problem by exercising the process.

Initially, we were treatingclosureas an administrative activity at the end of the session.

I now think it has a second, equally important purpose:

It prepares the next engineering session.

That changes how we should think about it.

The closure package is no longer just historical

It's actually thehandover packagefor the next startup.

The AIEMS Engineering Agent doesn't need the whole repository every time. It needs a curated set of authoritative artefacts that answer:

Where are we?

What is the current engineering objective?

What was completed?

What remains open?

What should I read first?

That means the closure process becomes the mechanism that prepares those answers.

I think the startup sequence should become

Instead of:

Repository?Search everything?Work out current state

The Agent should be able to do:

Repository?Read Programme Status (PST)?Read latest ESR?Read latest Engineering Summary / Handover?Confirm active objective?Continue

That is much more deterministic.

This also explains why we delayed closure

Earlier today you made an observation that I now think was very important:

Don't update closure artefacts throughout the session; do it once at the end.

At the time we discussed it as avoiding repeated updates.

Now I think there's another benefit.

The closure package becomes atrusted engineering baseline.

The Agent knows:

"These artefacts represent the final agreed state of the previous session."

It doesn't have to reconcile multiple intermediate updates.

Recommendation

Rather than changing the Agent again, I would improve theclosure workflow.

Its outputs should be explicitly designed asAgent startup artefacts.

For example:

PST-0001- current programme state.

Latest ESR- authoritative engineering record.

Engineering Session Summary / Handover- concise operational briefing.

Registers- updated once with accepted decisions.

Then the Agent startup instruction becomes very simple:

Read the current programme state, latest ESR, latest handover, then verify against the repository before beginning engineering.

I think we've completed the loop

At the beginning of ESR-0012, we were trying to teach me to remember.

At the end of ESR-0012, we've designed a system where the Agent doesn't need to remember.

It reads theauthoritative engineering stateprepared by the previous session.

That is a much stronger engineering model.

One final observation

I think we've just identified another subtle evolution of AIEMS.

Originally, I thought of the closure process as"record what happened."

I now think its primary purpose is:

"Prepare the next engineer."

It doesn't matter whether that next engineer is:

you,

me,

the AIEMS Engineering Agent,

Codex,

or, in the future, Guardian.

The closure artefacts become theengineering handover package.

I think that's a significant improvement in how we view the end of an engineering session, and it fits perfectly with the direction Project JARVIS AI is taking.

Received app response

Governance / Engineering - AIEMS closure process verified.

The repository doesnotsupport my earlier invented "WP6.1-WP6.5" closure structure as the established closure process.

The established AIEMS process says:

Before closing an Engineering Session, check whether AIEMS itself changed during the session.

If the engineering process changed, update and baseline the authoritative artefacts before closure.

Previous Engineering Sessions hand over to the next planned Engineering Session Report, but donotcreate it.

A new ESR is created during the next session's WP0B only after repository synchronisation confirms the previous session is formally closed.

For startup, AIEMS requires WP0A Repository Synchronisation and WP0B Engineering Session Initialisation, including README, PST-0001, latest accepted ESR, PBK-0001, COC-0001, history/full-chat artefacts, repository baseline, previous session status, and Programme Sponsor approval.

So the correct ESR-0012 closure path is simpler:

Closure Step 1:Check whether AIEMS changed during ESR-0012.Closure Step 2:If yes, prepare controlled updates to authoritative artefacts.Closure Step 3:Produce ESR-0012 closure / handover record.Closure Step 4:Baseline repository after approved updates.Closure Step 5:ESR-0013 starts later via WP0A/WP0B.

My earlier plan was too elaborate.
